B003EPPEP4|5.0|I begin this review of Nikki Yanofsky's second album, self-titled \\"Nikki\\" as I did my review of her first album, \\"Ella . . . Of Thee I Swing,\\" with a paragraph of instructions to you, the reader:    If you have bought \\"Nikki\\" and you do not have Nikki's first album (a combination CD and DVD album), set \\"Nikki\\" aside, buy her first album, and--well--watch the DVD from that first, then listen to \\"Nikki.\\"  Do that and you will understand the roots of this young, but already-so-experienced jazz singer.  If you are thinking of buying this album, I absolutely recommend--at the risk of sounding like Nikki Yanofsky's marketing manager--that you buy both of her albums and view and listen to them in the order that I set forth above.    Now, about \\"Nikki.\\"  I will freely admit that the jazz purist in me was ready to dislike some of this album.  Unlike her first album where, with the exception of one of the bonus studio tracks, she stuck to jazz standards and popular tunes penned over a half-century ago, Nikki ventures partially here into singing some pop tunes, most that she co-wrote.  Happily, also included are a healthy dose of more jazz standards.  Gone here, too, is the big band, replaced with various smaller instrumental groups, with and without backup singers.  About those musicians, their playing is impeccable, swinging where that is the mood called for--which, with Nikki's jazz chops, is pretty often.  Still, I miss her swinging that full big band--maybe that will return in a future album.  This is also purely a studio album.  It is mastered perfectly--but, again, while Nikki swings deliciously on some tunes in this album, I miss (and I suspect that she did, too) the electricity of her singing her heart out in front of a live audience.    I fully understand the reasoning behind how this album was put together.  Sad but true, jazz singers who sing just pure jazz may be musically true to themselves, but they usually labor as relative unknowns to all but a small cadre ofjazz aficionados.  We jazz purists may wince at a great young jazz talent such as Nikki (though she firmly contends that she is not just a jazz singer) broadening her scope to include some pop, but it may serve to bring her a wider audience, especially among her age peers, who then may discover that they actually like her jazz side--a lot--and therein some new jazz fans may be created.  Jazz critics at the time frequently growled about singers like Ella Fitzgerald, Frank Sinatra, or Sarah Vaughan \\"going commercial,\\" but a younger singer like Nikki would likely have never heard and been inspired by them had they not.    Briefly, a few highlights from this album:    Nikki brightly swings all of the jazz standards on this album.  After a somewhat \\"schmaltzy\\" syncopated introduction to Billy Strayhorn's tune written for Duke Ellington (and his long-time theme song), \\"Take the `A' Train,\\" Nikki launches into a lusciously swinging improvised solo.  Rework the intro into a swinging straight ahead arrangement and this tune would be near-perfect.    \\"You Have to Swing It (Mr. Paganini),\\" one of Ella Fitzgerald's hallmark tunes, is sung and swung here with the voice and musical maturity that removes any thoughts that Nikki is not a \\"jazz lion\\" ready to help carry jazz's torch into the future.    \\"Bienvenue Dans Ma Vie\\" is Nikki's co-written lightly swinging European-flavored jazz excursion--sung beautifully with both French and English verses.  The listener firmly can feel the influences of a Stephane Grapelli or Jean Luc-Ponty in this tune.    I will admit that \\"Somewhere Over the Rainbow\\" is not my favorite tune to hear--it has been sung far too much--often by singers who butcher it thoroughly.  When Nikki sang it on her first album, though, she sang it lovingly and emotionally as a jazz standard that was a highlight of that concert.  She reprises it here, with only piano accompaniment, still as a tender jazz ballad.  Comparing both versions, the listener realizes how muchthis singer's voice, already lovely at age 13, has matured physically in three years.      Which brings me to my last highlight:  Nikki's singing of her co-written tribute song to her idol, Ella Fitzgerald, \\"First Lady.\\"  What we only heard fleetingly in her first album comes to full flower in this tune.  Here we treated to the mournful, sorrowful, and the darker side of Nikki's singing--and what a musically captivating moment it is.  Some of Nikki's critics have opined that Nikki's young age and lack of \\"life experience\\" renders her incapable of singing ballads with the emotional maturity that those tunes require.  Well, this tune should dispel those notions.  We can only hope that Nikki's future releases will include a healthy share of jazz standard ballads.  Her rapidly growing emotional depth, combined with her ever-smoothing lovely voice would make that a real musical treat.    Finally, if there is any \\"immaturity\\" or irony in Nikki Yanofsky, it comes, not in her album, but when she lists some of the current pop stars whose works she admires.  In most cases, my response to her about those statements is this:  \\"Honey, you left them in the dust long ago--they could learn a lot from you.\\"

0451205766|4.0|I don't know how I never read \\"The Godfather\\" before. Maybe because I'd seen the movie so many times and hadn't loved other Puzo novels. But for no particular reason I picked it up last week and couldn't put it down. My reading experience was fully colored by the film - I saw the actors and heard their voices on the page - and having read the book changed my film viewing - Coppola seemed even more brilliant for distilling complexity to a few lines, a few quick edits (it also helped me understand parts of the film I had puzzled over, like the close up of Michael's hand outside the hospital). A wonderful synergy.    Fabulous: the first half or so is riveting. The characters are vivid, the plots complex, the dialog convincing, the locations rooted in the details of senses, and I will long be digesting (and writing about) Vito's philosophy of Leadership. The leisurely pace, digressions, and frequent introduction of new colorful characters reminds me of the best 19th century British novels.    Flawed: Puzo wasn't paying attention if anyone ever told him \\"show, don't tell.\\" He beats us over the head with repetitive descriptions of characters' qualities and motivations. He lacks Vito's fine appreciation of subtlety, slapping us with the obvious. Too many characters are not developed sufficiently, and then prove to be either superfluous (what's the point of the doctor and Lucy?) or too easily disposed of (were we supposed to care about Moe Green?). Meanwhile, truly major characters - the Tattaglias, Barzini, the baddies so necessary for the finale - are little more than names on the page.    In fact, the book loses much of its energy in the second half. Compare the fine vignettes of Sollozzo and Woltz near the beginning with the complete failure to make Barzini a foe worth reckoning with at the end. Where we bite our nails when Michael takes on Sollozzo, we don't break a sweat as he prepares to tie up all the loose ends; the build up to the finale lacks tension, the outcomefeels inevitable. It's like Puzo couldn't quite figure out where he was going for a long time, and when he figured it out, never went back to edit and dial up the drama.    Finally, I found most of the sex scenes uninteresting and unnecessary.     Still, I read this book with the greatest pleasure and will be recommending it to friends for a long time.

Griffith, who showed that the audience was capable of following two and three plots within a movie (Birth of a Nation), Tron was truly one of the first steps toward combining technology and storyline that challenged not only the intelligence, but the eye's ability to absorb truly visual interpretation.The only ones who saw what was coming were the geeks and pinheads, and we bought the VHS, the Disc (yeah, the big album looking one) and for a short period of time, you could get it on DVD for 5 bucks at Blockbuster with a free popcorn, coke and a box of candy!<br /><br />We bought the DVD, and told the clerk that we would have bought this without the frilly food. The clerk shrugged and said with a smirk, \\"Really?\\"<br /><br />I'm looking forward to seeing the new version, but I'm also expecting them to do their magic on the old one, making the shiny \\"shiny\\" and images awesome! If any film deserved it, this one does.

B003Q6D25K|4.0|I saw PA2 in the theater and was surprised how good it was -- an uncommonly effective sequel/prequel to the first PA film, which was a surprise in itself.<br /><br />What sets both films apart from so many so-called horror films today is the old-school less-is-more approach, perhaps necessitated on the first film by the shoe-string budget.<br /><br />Sound effects count for as much as visual effects, and there is barely a drop of blood to be seen anywhere.<br /><br />Instead, both films build suspense and terror the old-fashioned way -- slowly.  If you go back and watch the great horror films in cinema, you will see how carefully the terror is modulated.  The first big scares in \\"Alien\\" or \\"Psycho,\\" to name just two classics, don't appear until at least thirty minutes into the film.<br /><br />At that point, you're fully invested in the characters and the situation and wondering what will come next.<br /><br />The two PA films also rely on the static video cameras monitoring much of the paranormal activity.  This technique forces viewers to pay close attention to the screen, to see the scares, which start small and gradually ramp up.  When you hear a low-frequency rumble on the soundtrack, it's demon time... Stop shoveling popcorn in your mouth and pay closer attention!<br /><br />I have deducted a star for the lack of extras on the DVD.  There is an extended version of the film -- which I watched, but couldn't distinguish from the version I saw in the theater.  There are also a few extra \\"found scenes\\", one of which is genuinely scary and worth seeing.  But there is no director's commentary or making-of featurette, the absence of which I will ascribe more to studio greed than an adherence to the \\"found footage\\" illusion of this film and its predecessor.  (And that illusion is blown any way as soon as the lengthy technical credits roll at the end of the second film, shot on a larger budget.)<br /><br />So if you like suspenseful old-school horror films -- not the sadistic blood baths so common today -- see the first Paranormal Activity film and then the second.  You may have trouble sleeping afterwards, especially if you live in a house with a rumbling furnace!normal Activity film and then the second. 0790729377|5.0|I have seen this movie over the decades of my life as I grew from a naive and innocent teenager to a blossoming twenty something, then a mature thirtyish, the wisdom of my forties  and now an aging baby boomer.  In each part of my life I saw this movie through different eyes.  Now I have come back to it once more, with much life experience, heartache and losse and have an even deeper appreciation for this brilliant movie.    Elia Kazan manages to make the transition from the stage play that he directed to this scintillating movie version with almost all of the original cast intact.  The casting of Vivien Leigh as Blanche Dubois was the only change, due to the necessity of having a marquee name to get the movie made.  Karl Malden and Kim Hunter were Broadway stage actors and unknowns in Hollywood.  Marlon Brando had only made one previous film, \\"The Men\\", but was also still unknown to movie audiences.  Of course, all that would change after the world saw his mesmerizing, fiery, sexually raw, intense portrayal of Stanley Kowalski.  In the hands of a lesser actor, Stanley Kowalski could have come off as a two-dimensional, cardboard cartoonish caricature of a neanderthal brute.  However, in Brando's hands, he comes across as a multi-layered, complex, ambivalent, mercurial man who can change in a heartbeat from a wife-beating drunken brute to a lost, needy, vulnerable, childlike man who has a surprising tenderness at the most unexpected times.  This is the genius of Brando.  One never knows quite what to expect from one scene to the next and this adds to the already tense, tightly wound, emotionally charged atmosphere.      Vivien Leigh's Blanche Dubois is a faded southern belle, desperately clinging to a lost past, an aristocratic upbringing on the family estate Belle Rive, a life that is dead and gone forever.  Blanche has created an artificial world of fantasy and illusion to hide how lonely and desolate her reality has become.  She has never gotten over the trauma offalling in love with a young man who betrayed her love.  She turned on him cruelly and he killed himself. Blanche is still haunted by this tragedy and has never really been able to get beyond it.  She seems to be a woman incapable of coping with life's tragedies and losses, the setbacks and difficulties that one encounters along the way.  She must pretend to be that ageless southern belle, but in reality she has stooped so low as to seduce a seventeen year old student, causing her to be fired from her job as an english teacher.  After the loss of Belle Rive she took up residence at a second rate hotel and essentially prostitued herself with a long line of strange men, until finally she was asked to leave.  She has fallen a long way, but is determined to keep up the fragile facade of her past, holding a tenuous grip on reality.      Stanley is Blanche's nemesis, the one who discovers the truth about her past. It's interesting how my view of Stanley and Blanche has changed over the years.  I can now understand why Stanley unmasked Blanche and ended her chance of marrying his best friend Mitch.  After the scene in which Stanley beats Stella in a drunken tirade and causes her to run to the upstairs neighbor, but finally come back home, the next morning he overhears a conversation between Blanche and Stella. He hears Blanche describe him as \\"common\\", a \\"survivor of the stone age\\" and say that she has a plan to get them both out of there.  Once Stanley realizes that Blanche is a threat to his very survival, to his territory, his family, then there is no question that he will do whatever he must to destroy her.  The battle lines are drawn between Stanley and Blanche and Stella and her conflicting loyalties are caught right in the middle. However, as I watch this movie today, I see that it is inevitable that Blanche will lose this epic battle.  She is no match for Stanley, who is tough, uncompromising, strong, determined and outraged at this woman who he sees as a two-faced, pretentious hypocrite without a job, money and totally destitute.  I also believe that Stanley is aware enough to realize Blanche's precarious mental state.  He senses that she is on the edge, a woman on the verge of a nervous breakdown.  All he has to do is help it along.      I no longer see Blanche as a victim.  I feel empathy for her plight and her slow descent into madness.  It's not a pretty site.  However, I can also see that her lies, constructed out of her own necessity to keep up this sham existence, have also caused pain and hurt to others. I can understand Stanley telling his friend Mitch the truth, rather than stand by and watch him make a fool of himself. I don't believe that Mitch or anyone else can ever save Blanche from her fate.  At one point in the movie, after the birthday party for Blanche in which Mitch has failed to show up because of what Stanley told him, we see Stanley and Stella arguing heatedly.  Stella asks what will Blanche do now if Mitch won't marry her.  Stanley utters a line which seems to be a precursor of Blanche's fate.  He says - \\"Oh, her future is all mapped out for her\\". He gives her a ticket back to Oriole and Blanche is devastated and Stella is angered at this callous gesture.      When I watched this movie earlier in my life, I actually felt as someone else did here in their review. I wasn't sure if Stanley raped Blanche or not.  That scene that passed for a rape back in 1951, seems somewhat ambiguous now.  I never read the play and now I wish that I had done so a long time ago.  It would have cleared up a good deal of my confusion.  I totally understand the constraints in which the creators of this movie were working back in the days of strict censorship.  There are controversial and taboo subjects in this story and some of it had to be removed or at least watered down. I have researched the play online and know the actual story as Williams originally wrote it.  Stanley does rape Blanche in a final act of domination andcontrol.  In doing so, he pushes her over the edge and the last vestiges of her sanity are destroyed.      What I have also come to understand, is that Tennessee Williams didn't write a story about good versus evil, right versus wrong.  This is not a morality tale.  This is reality versus illusion.  Stanley represents the modern world in all its raw coarseness, survival of the fittest.  Blanche represents a bygone world, a halcyon existence that has faded into the past, a world of frivolity, elegance, gentility and grace.  She cannot adapt to this new world and retreats into a world of make-believe.  After the scene in which Mitch angrily confronts Blanche and leaves, having found out the truth for himself, we see Blanche alone in the darkened apartment. She has gotten all dressed up with a tiara and ballgown and is conversing with imaginary guests, retreating into a long ago night of partying at Belle Rive. She is once more the southern belle, humming along to imaginary music.  Her reverie is interrupted by Stanley arriving home from the hospital.  The two of them will spend the night alone there together.  The final confrontation will inevitably take place in the ugliest possible way.  Their destinies will collide one last time and, when it is over, Blanche will have gone completely out of her mind.      The original ending was changed at the insistence of the censors, but I believe that they didn't achieve what they intended. Somehow Kazan filmed the ending in such an ambiguous way with Stella running upstairs to the neighbor vowing that this time she won't ever go back. It isn't convincing, not with what we know of her relationship with Stanley.  I always felt that she never really left for good.  Now that I know the real ending, it's almost as though I knew what Tennessee Williams had in mind without ever reading the play.  The censors just succeeded in muddying everything, clouding it with doubt.  That's why the ending never really made sense to me.  I thinkthat people will come away with their own ideas about the ending.  The triumph is that this movie got made at all.  The basic story is still intact, the actors stayed faithful to their characters and Kazan's brilliant direction made it all come together in the end.      Truth has a way of coming through even when some are determined to try to cover it up.  The censors didn't really change the ending.  They didn't take away a powerful story of passion, betrayal, deceit, lust, brutal truth and a climactic battle that ended inevitably.  This movie has stood the test of time and is as powerful and unsettling and relevant today as it was sixty years ago.  Everyone owes it to themselves to watch true greatness.

0061743526|5.0|A marvellous story told from the perspective of a ten year old girl growing up in a small town in Alabama. Does any other story capture so vividly the perspective of a young child?<br /><br />The story is roughly divided into three parts. The first is about the narrator Scout (Jean Louise), her older brother, and their young friend Dill. We follow them as they explore their house, neighbourhood with assorted and eclectic neighbours, and their father Atticus. Gradually the focus shifts to the trial where their father is to be the defence attorney. This is the centerpiece of the book - with strong passages about racial prejudice and intolerance. It is blatantly obvious that their town has two levels of justice - one for the dominant white people and another for the black people. Woe to the black person who transgresses any part of this racial divide (in this case a sexual one) - for \\"justice\\" will be served.<br /><br />By taking a child's perspective the full inequality of the system is brought out. The trial also serves notice that even if a poor white man is \\"victimized\\" by this racial code the black person still has no chance. Scout, her brother Jem, and Dill sit in the segregated black area of the court. Dill's feelings during the trial are most eloquent.<br /><br />After the trial the book seems to lose momentum somewhat - the events after seem anti-climatic. The attempted murder of Scout and Jem by Ewell is somewhat contrived and mundane compared to what has taken place - so the ending is rather weak.<br /><br />Nevertheless this is still an American Classic. The childhood viewpoint makes this an ornate and delightful read.

The drug unexpectedly drives them mad and mutates their own bodies (radiation mutation is another 1950s fear) before finally killing them. A subordinate likewise condemns Professor Deemer to death and mutation by injecting him with the formula as they scuffle among caged test animals in Deemer's laboratory. To literally illustrate 1950s fear of experiments escaping containment and wreaking havoc, one experiment, an oversized tarantula, walks to freedom out a broken door as the laboratory is destroyed by the fire.<br /><br />As with most B 1950s sci-fi movies the standard of acting and character development is subordinate to the plot, so it would be unfair to comment on the acting of John Agar and Mara Corday.<br />There is no point either, in mentioning that the spider ultimate height of 100' utterly defies the square/cube principle.<br /><br />The spider meets its doom at the hands of an very young actor who went on to huge TV and movie stardom...but to say any more would create an unforgivable spoiler.<br /><br />Tarantula is a B movie,after all, where suspension of disbelief is at its greatest, and  intended as prelude to the A movie the audience actually came to see.<br />Jack Arnold's thrust about nuclear radiation and scientists loosing control of the experiments and putting humanity at risk is purely allegorical. B002BSHXJA|5.0|Being almost 35 years old, and having missed The Beatles when they first came out, I missed out on some things.  However, one thing I remember well is listening to their original 45's handed down from family - and these were always mono.  This was the way The Beatles intended it to sound.  The Beatles themselves only personally supervised the MONO mixes of all their albums before \\"Magical Mystery Tour\\".  The stereo mixes were just there, frankly, to satisfy audio snobs who, at the time, refused to listen to anything that wasn't stereo.  However, when The Beatles themselves would hold a listening party for one of their new albums, they always presented the mono version, because you couldn't hear the whole sonic picture of the song unless you stood directly in the middle of the two speakers.  Needless to say, very few people can stand in such a position at the same time.  There was one story of Paul playing a stereo version of a certain album for a friend, and they had to keep walking back and forth between the left and right speakers to hear either Ringo's drums, George's guitar, and so on.  This wasn't the case with the standard mono 45's and mono LP's.  I remember the crisp, exciting sound of these records, replete with hisses, pops, and scratches.  When I got older I, of course, still loved The Fab Four and bought all of their releases in various formats...well, all of the formats to be precise (including 8track).  I must say, and I am in no way being a revisionist, I always thought that many of their recordings in stereo sounded awkward.  The panning was too much!  George's guitar on the right, Paul's bass on the left, even the harmonies were panned in some recordings.  I tried various things, like sitting directly in the middle of my speakers trying to feel the full effect of the recordings.  I was always very 'aware' of the separation left to right.  Well, as I sit here now blissfully listening to \\"Lucy in the Sky with Diamonds\\" from \\"Sergent Pepper\\" in mono, Ifeel my life as a Beatles fan is now complete.<br />  This is how I always dreamed I could listen to my favorite four British Blokes.  I'm not kidding, I remember clearly driving down the road at age 16, listening to the blue compilation on cassette, cringing at the separation and wishing that they would release a better mix someday.  Now I have it!  These selected albums re-released in glorious mono is a godsend to a Beatles nut like me.  I haven't listened to every cd from the set yet, but I'm closing in.  The ones I have listened to, \\"Beatles For Sale\\", \\"Hard Days Night\\", \\"Help\\", \\"Rubber Soul\\", \\"Revolver\\", and \\"Sergent Pepper\\" ALL sound better in mono.  Not only do we have these titles thankfully available in mono mixes, but the mastering of the sound quality is mind boggling.  The only word I can use to describe the mastering of these cd's is \\"perfect\\".  I rarely call anything perfect.<br />  I own the complete Beatles boxed set (in stereo), and the Beatles USB.  Of course I have every video they've ever made.  I now have it all.  And I must say, these mono masters are the keystone in a beautiful Beatles collection.  If you don't like The Beatles, but LOVE The Beatles - BUY THIS before it's too late.  It is, after all, a limited edition.  Now let me hear some of that rock and roll music!

1449382673|5.0|Head First Python is an introduction to the Python language for programmers with a background in other languages.   By the end of the first chapter, you'll have experienced lists, functions and iteration, while moving forward at a comfortable pace.   You will not find detailed API descriptions and edge case discussions, and the book doesn't bog down in esoteric examples.    Programming \\"best practices\\" are not neglected, either.   Code re-use and modules are quickly brought into the discussion, and then on to basic exception handling.  I found that to be a refreshing change from most programming texts,  which seem to expend lots of ink on weird exceptions or the author's favorite math problems, then append slim chapters at the end of the work mentioning techniques that should have been used \\"in the real world\\".    Most code worked as written, and there are active errata pages available.  For the book's target audience, I suspect the failures I encountered would not present any issues.    Overall, an enjoyable read and well worth the time taken to work through the exercises.  I'll be using this at work as the basis of our internal training for one of our support groups which is transitioning from Perl to Python.      Disclaimer: I received access to an electronic copy of this book from O'Reilly Publishing for the purpose of writing this review.

0143118706|5.0|First off, this book is not for everyone. As other reviewers have suggested, it is not the same kind of tale as Eat, Pray, Love. It is not just a memoir like the first book, but a reflection on what marriage is and what makes it successful.     What I really loved about this was how different Elizabeth's Gilbert's experiences and thoughts were from my own. I can relate to the love of travel and the desire to bring peace, understanding, and love into her life. But I couldn't relate to her intense fear of marriage, as it is described in the book.  (I married well into my thirties and, having no prior bad experiences, wanted to marry my guy with all my heart.) So it was a fascinating read for me, listening to her work her way through her fear through careful research on the institution of marriage. She peppers her newfound research and epiphanies throughout the tale of her and Felipe's wandering the globe while waiting for U.S. paperwork to go through.     She has this ideal and very adult relationship with Felipe, and you can't help admiring this couple as they pull back each onion layer and get to know each other more deeply. And of course, to get to that next layer, you've got the metaphorical tears and pain. It's rarely neat and pretty. Each has his or her moments of frustration and \\"hissy fits,\\" but the subsequent revelations make their bond that much stronger.    If you think about marriage and what makes it tick/work/flourish or if you wanted to know what happened next in Gilbert's tale, you will most likely enjoy this book. If you want a straight story like Eat, Pray, Love, then this book may not satisfy your craving.

Seeing a big monster jump out and go 'boo' was more appealing to me than watching a bunch of space ships flying around zapping each other out of the sky.    Well, I'm still not a huge sci-fi fan, but I have come to embrace the Star Wars franchise {mainly the original trilogy} The stories that revolved around the original characters Luke, Hans and Princess Lea were engrossing, exciting and deeply moving. They were like a throwback to those great fantasy movies from the early sixties like 7th Voyage of Sinbad, Jason and the Argonauts, and the Magic Sword. My reason for writing this review is to share my feelings about Mr. Lucas, and his constant need to tinker with his movies. The scene as it was originally shot played well enough. We fans don't need to hear Vader break down like a wimp in a terribly dubbed voice to know that he couldn't bear to see his beloved son suffer an excruitiatingly painful death at the hands of a man he didn't particularly like to begin with. I hate it when a filmmaker thinks so little of the intelligence of his audience that he feels that he has to create dialogue that explains every little scene that is showed.    In other words, if Lucas and 20th Century Fox truly want to create the perfect Star Wars dvd franchise, than they should concentrate thier efforts in putting out an ultimate edition with all the documentaries, deleted scenes, interviews and trailers that are available, and bundle them all in one final boxset. Then Lucas and the folks at Fox could spend the rest of their lives counting their profits and at the same time have a clear concience. If that day ever comes, then may the force be with them!

044024367X|1.0|I have been reading each of Lee Childs' books in order, and to be honest, I am not sure what all the fuss is about.<br /><br />His writing is so-so, and his \\"mysteries\\" obvious and predictable. If you are interested in a truly great mystery writer, try Michael Connelly's Harry Bosch detective series, and if you like more violent action, Robert Crais' Joe Pike character.<br /><br />His lack of understanding of the military mind is irritating. Childs needs to study the American military subculture, as he makes so many obviously wrong statements on the general views most military officers share on politics and religion.<br /><br />As a retired career Air Force military officer and pilot who also spent a lot of time with the Army, I think Childs does not understand what a member of the American military officer corps thinks like or believes.<br /><br />This book was especially \\"out there\\", having Reacher (raised by a Marine father and a former MP himself) strongly support deserting from the service because he (Childs, obviously) disagrees with conservative politics and the war in Iraq. Talk about unrealistic?  He completely lost me from the book at that point.<br /><br />He also throughout the series makes a big deal out of an MP officer being \\"the baddest of the Bad\\" and trained to be an over the top street fighter.  Not so much.<br /><br />I like for a book to draw me in to its world, make me believe the things in it could really happen.  When an author is so heavy handed that his characters become preachy and excessively talky about ideas their character type would never espouse without the author's intervention, he loses me and my interest.<br /><br />His take on religion was also offensive, and his use of the term \\"Anglican\\" for one of the religious figures in the book was also wrong-- in America, this denomination is generally called Episcopal.  The character's description of the process of the writing of the Book of Revelation also is absolutely in error, and reflects Child's uneducated opinion of the process of early Christian writings becoming part of the Canon of the Bible.  (For the truth, read \\"From God to Us: How We Got Our Bible\\", by Dr Norman Geisler and William Nix)This is to be expected, I guess, when Childs tries to make Reacher the \\"smarter\\" atheist whenever he comes up against a person of faith, who is without exception psychotic, brutal, stupid, two-faced, corrupt, venal or weak.<br /><br />Childs needs to have someone edit his writing better for Anglicisms versus Americanisms, also.  Every book has some awkward Anglicisms in it that jar one out of immersion in the story-- how many Americans know what a \\"spanner\\" is?  If I hadn't owned a Jaguar in my misspent youth I wouldn't.  Or how many Americans know \\"binned\\" means thrown in the trash (\\"rubbish bin\\"), and isn't a misspelling of \\"bent\\"?<br /><br />I am also pretty tired of his obligatory sex scene in each new book.  His earlier books handled the subject in a more effective, understated way.  The sex scenes in his later books seem to be aimed at the teenage boy audience, with way more explicit detail than is necessary to get the point across.<br /><br />Childs' Reacher books have become so predictable in so many ways, they remind me of the formulaic Louis L'Amour westerns-- when the pretty girl is introduced early in the book, you know Jack will end up sleeping with her several times;  when the evil boss-man shows up, you know Reacher will beat him up at the minimum and most likely kill him;  when the big enforcer thug-type shows up, you know Reacher will beat him within an inch of his life or kill him.  The \\"big mystery\\" of each new book will be obvious early on if you pay attention to details, and from that point on, you might as well save yourself time and skim the pages until you get to the end.  There's always plenty of fluff you wouldn't need to waste your time on-- it adds nothing to the story line.  For instance, in this book Childs brings up many times that \\"Reacher knew that the average time for someone to answer their front door in the evening is 20 seconds.  Twenty three seconds later the front door opened.\\"  Oh, come on now.<br /><br />This book finally hit my gag reflex on so many levels.  I'm done spending my money on Childs' books.  If only Robert Crais or Michael Connelly could write faster!

I  had already heard before something was wrong with these models their assumptions on prices following a normal probability distribution, but not exactly what. Like Mandelbrot says, it's a house of cards.<br /><br />Most helpful in this book are the explanations from the author on what a normal distribution is, how Gauss developed his probability curve and how it's widely used in a lot of scientific fields. The movie shows us the prejudice and discrimination norms against Indians in not only India but in South Arica, both controlled by the British Empire. These immoral acts against fellow man sparked Gandhi's lifelong journey to bring peace among all races and eventually the independence of India from British rule.  The story brings to light the personal sacrifice people are willing to make in an attempt to bring peace to a race or culture that is seen as inferior to another.  There are scenes that are similar in nature to events that took place during Martin Luther King's era and the African American's march to freedom.   The story begins with a 24-year old Gandhi riding a train in first class.  After being confronted for being an Indian in first class he is thrown off the train, even though he is a London educated lawyer.  He then realizes that there are several South African laws that are biased against Indians and decides to take a stand.  He begins a non-violent protest campaign which quickly draws followers and popularity among Indians in South Africa.  After numerous arrests and the unwanted attention of the world, the British government gives in and gives right to Indians.  He is seen as a hero in India and is invited back to his homeland to aid in the fight for independence.   After returning home to India he takes time to travel and witnesses the state of poverty that his people in are.  He agrees to help fight for freedom and starts another non-violent, non-cooperating civil protest campaign.  This time around seems to be more difficult, however, given the millions of Indians nationwide and the uncertainty that all will conform to such non-violent ways.  There are several setbacks including extreme violence against the protestors and the occasional imprisonment of Gandhi himself.  Nevertheless, thecampaign generates huge attention from all over the world the British government come under great public pressure.  After World War II, Britain is too weak to continue controlling India and grants the country independence.  Gandhi is now seen as the father of India and is greatly admired.   Even though Indians are now free and able to establish their own country, they have many tasks including keeping peace between Hindus and Muslims.  After much discussion, parts of the former western British India, where Muslims were the majority, were separated into Pakistan.  This is something that Gandhi did not agree to because he wanted all races to live together peacefully.  After India and Pakistan were separated into their own countries, violence between Muslims and Hindus erupted through all of the two countries.  Equally so millions of people between both cultures were displaced and forced to move into either country.  This disturbed Gandhi deeply and declares he will fast until all fighting stops.  He does so and almost to the point of death.  This causes violence, at least on the side of the Hindus, to slowly cease.  There is a scene where an angry Hindu man approaches a very weary Gandhi.  He states that the violence has stopped and tosses a piece of bread onto Gandhi demanding he eat it.  The man then admits to killing a Muslim child because Muslims had killed his son.  Gandhi tells the man in order to avoid hell raise a child, whom Muslim parents are dead, and raise him as a Muslim.   Peace exists between the Muslims and Hindus and Gandhi returns to his motivated loving ways.  It seems that most people who are successful in bringing some form of peace leave this earth too soon, as is the case with Gandhi.  In the movie he talks about how ruthless, murderous rulers die and their ideas with them.  The only philosophy that lives on is peace and love for everyone.  Gandhi is shot at point blank range three times by angry Hindu doing the work of someone else.  Though Gandhi is murdered, his ideas of peace live on.  Peace always wins.    Steven M. Christin

While most Robin Hood media focus on his time in Sherwood Forest tormenting the Sheriff of Nottingham with his steal-from-the-rich antics, this movie actually tells the tale of how Robin Longstride, a common archer in the King's army, eventually became the outlaw known as Robin Hood. Disappointingly, they never really say how he managed to become such an incredible archer. He already seems to have mastered that art before the start of the movie. The movie was terrifically cast, gorgeously costumed and designed, and overall very enjoyable to watch.    In short, I'd say it was one of the more underrated movies of its year, and it deserved way more credit than it got!
B000RZIGVS|2.0|Well, I purchased a copy from a very well-reviewed marketplace seller on here, and sure enough - it was a bootleg version.    Since I contacted the seller and they seemed to be as legitimately shocked as I was that they were selling bootlegs, I promised them I would not reveal who they were here on the promise that they would stop selling their copies immediately and contact their wholesaler.    Anyway, there's a few things to look for that will indicate you have the bootleg:    - The insert and poster are missing from the set    - The discs are packaged in lime-green (instead of transparant green) casings with two discs on the same side (Paramount's official version has one disc on the front/one disc on the back of each casing).  The bootleg discs also have plastic sleeves over each DVD.  While Paramount's casings are molded together (much like the Voyager season sets and other releases), the bootleg copies are merely held together with some tape.    - The bootleg discs are single-layered, while the legit discs are dual-layered (you can check this on your computer...if a disc says it has 4.7 GB of info (or 4.8 GB), you've got yourself a bootleg!)    - Finally, some posters have claimed that if the back (shiny part!) of your DVD says \\"STAR TRER\\" around the inner circle, you have the bootleg.  This may be true, but mine DID say \\"STAR TREK\\" and were the bootlegs...so this typo seems to have been fixed by those sending out the faux versions!    Hope this info is helpful...does anyone know/can confirm that Amazon sells the LEGIT sets.  Would like to hear from those who bought directly from Amazon and see if they got the bootleg copies as well.

0470913029|4.0|The Paleo Diet is another version of a low-carb diet that focuses what ancient man (cavemen) probably ate.  The author contends that the human body was created to eat those kinds of foods and will be healthiest as a result.    What I liked about this book is that what is allowed is healthy: lean meat, fish, and seafood; some nuts and a little \\"good\\" fat, fruit and non-starchy vegetables, and no processed or refined foods.  The author makes the case that most of our modern diseases, including allergies and digestive problems, are the result of processed foods and ingredients that are non-foods never meant for human consumption, calling the American diet a nutritional nightmare. I agree. He also promotes eating local, organically raised and grown meats and produce, something that makes sense if they are available and affordable.    However, anyone considering this diet should know that it is quite strict and eliminates all cereals, grains (including rice) and flours and anything made with them; legumes (beans and peanuts), dairy products, and salt.  That may make this plan unrealistic for many people to follow in the long-term, as usually happens when a diet eliminates complete food groups.    I know from personal experience that a low-carb diet works for people like me who are insulin resistant so as plan to kick-start weight loss, get back on track nutritionally, and to help reverse things like metabolic syndrome, high blood pressure and high cholesterol, this book has good information.  While I don't see it as a plan I could realistically stick with for long periods at a time as presented, I do like the book as a reference and came away with ideas that will help me with a low-carb diet.

1594202842|5.0|This is a book about a mother's love for her daughters and her family. As a story of one family's challenges and triumphs, it's a vivid and moving portrayal of the values that come from having immigrant parents. Chua, the daughter of Chinese immigrants, was raised in a loving home but one that demanded hard work and discipline -- values she saw her own parents exhibit in spades as they made a new life in America. The strength Chua cultivated in herself because her parents had high expectations enabled her to recognize her own children's strength, too. Her love for Sophia and Lulu meant giving them the same gift of resilience. As a mother, Chua was in the trenches: she went to all the recitals and lessons, wrote (unbelievably) detailed notes for her daughter's practice routines, had an insane schedule flying across the country, and put in hours of hard work to make sure her daughters did the same. This is not the story of a \\"show parent\\" setting high expectations and sitting back. For Chua, you practice what you preach. And when Chua's sister fell ill, and when her mother-in-law was ill, her daughters saw Chua practice patience, compassion, and, above all, love and loyalty to family.      Further, Chua didn't just set high expectations for performance in music or school. She had high expectations for her daughters as people. When her mother-in-law passed, she made sure she did everything she could so that her daughters wrote heartfelt and moving statements. She took them on world travels and exposed them to other cultures, pushing them to try new experiences. She returned the quickly fashioned birthday cards her daughters gave her -- because she took her obligations to her family seriously, she expected her children to do the same. And, after that, they did.       This is such a vital contribution to the national dialogue because Chua's values are at heart the ones that America was founded on: strength and toughness, but also basic goodness and compassion. Love too often today is equated with permissiveness. Chua knows that love also means recognizing that strength, skill, and good character don't arise merely from knowing that you're loved and affirmed -- they arise from determination, hard work and actually being tested by the world. Whatever others say, it's more than clear by the end of the book that Sophia and Lulu can lay claim to that.     This book has changed the way I will think about parenting when I start my family.

006097625X|5.0|This is a book on comics \\"theory\\", as in \\"music theory\\". It is exactly what the title claims: a breakdown and analysis of what comics do, as a communication medium, and how they do it. McCloud examines comics as they are now and demonstrates-believably-how comics operate in ways that text alone cannot. For example, it isn't a history of comics or a how-to book for aspiring comics artists, but McCloud does show different ways of drawing the same thing, and points out how each different style carries a slightly different message for the reader. So the same aspiring comics artist won't learn how to draw from this book, but he or she could learn how better to make the point he or she is trying to make. This book is a serious, thoughtful, clear, and well-researched essay presented as a comic book.

B00328G4V6|4.0|All the complaining about this release that I've been reading is pretty absurd. Even if this actually WAS a cynical ploy to milk money from fans with sub-par material, this is JIMI HENDRIX. Everyone who releases music would like it to sell and make money and this one is no different. C'mon people. It's always worth hearing Hendrix play guitar, whether it's an alternate version of a well-known song, a studio outtake, a rehearsal or even just noodling around. This is likely the greatest guitar player who's ever lived and ever will. Think Hendrix was a perfectionist who never would've allowed these songs to see the light of day? Well, maybe he'd feel differently now that he's gone but we'll never know will we?<br /><br />To be sure, he DID strive to achieve perfection and he was never completely satisfied, particularly with his first two albums. But they were released anyways and we can all be grateful for that. The public demand to hear what's in the vaults exists for a good reason - even if it doesn't measure up to his best work, this music is well worth hearing.<br /><br />Breakdown:<br /><br />\\"Stone Free\\" - This is the first original song Jimi wrote for the Experience and it was used on the B-side of his first single, \\"Hey Joe\\". The version presented here is arguably better than the original release. It's funkier - the drums have more swing, the guitars are more energetic and the guitar solo is scorching. New bassist Billy Cox replaces Noel Redding and greatly improves the bass lines with dexterity and added muscle. The whole song is more organic. The only minor drawback is the ending where the song loses its momentum and becomes a bit repetitive. ****1/2<br /><br />\\"Valleys of Neptune\\" - This song was still a work in progress before Jimi's untimely death, but you wouldn't necessarily know that just from listening to it. The guitar parts exemplify the sophisticated direction Hendrix was heading toward in 1969 and the song itself is gorgeous. ****<br /><br />\\"Bleeding Heart\\" - Jimi covers an old song from blues guru and Hendrix influence Elmore James. It's a bit rough, particularly the vocals, but it's quite spirited and Jimi's guitar is on fire. ****1/2<br /><br />\\"Hear My Train A-Coming\\" - is a classic blues penned by Hendrix. According to the liner notes, this version was recorded live in the studio in just one take. It definitely has a loose, jam session feel to it. Fantastic! *****<br /><br />\\"Mr. Bad Luck\\" - This is the earliest recorded track of the bunch - 1967. It was made during the AXIS:BOLD AS LOVE sessions and was understandably discarded although it's still a good song. It's a straight-up rocker that any fan of Hendrix's guitar playing should appreciate. ***1/2<br /><br />\\"Sunshine of Your Love\\" - This is a very solid instrumental version of the classic Cream song, using the arrangement created by The Experience for the stage. It's not the best version I've heard, but it's virtually flawless and Hendrix still blows Clapton out of the water. The middle of the song gets quiet and subdued which isn't exactly engaging, but it allows the band to build up to a climax before once again launching into that classic riff. ****1/4<br /><br />\\"Lover Man\\" - is derived from BB King's \\"Rock Me Baby,\\" a song Hendrix positively TORE UP at the famous Monterrey Pop Festival. This track, to my mind, is only notable for Hendrix's guitar playing. Quite frankly, I got tired of this standard 12-bar blues chord progression ages ago, but it's worth hearing in the hands of a guitarist as great as Hendrix is. ***1/2<br /><br />\\"Ships Passing Through The Night\\" - a song that's a little off the beaten path, kinda funky with angular guitar figures. As always, it's all about Hendrix's guitar and there's an unusual little guitar freak out at the end of the song. Recorded in 1969, this is another track that highlights the way he was playing towards the end of his life, especially on \\"Night Bird Flying\\" which this song resembles in certain ways.****<br /><br />\\"Fire\\" - Recorded in 1969, this is a well done if not particularly remarkable blast through the ARE YOU EXPERIENCED? classic from 1967, save for the red hot Hendrix solo. It's looser than the album version, thanks especially to the confident, swinging and colorful playing of drummer Mitch Mitchell in a style he developed in live settings. ****<br /><br />\\"Red House\\" - a fine, extended reading of Hendrix's classic blues original. ****1/2<br /><br />\\"Lullaby for the Summer\\" - is a cool, rangey instrumental that would eventually evolve into \\"Ezy Rider.\\" Stands as a unique piece of studio experimentation. ***1/2<br /><br />\\"Crying Blue Rain\\" - begins as a soulful instrumental that picks up the pace towards the end of the song. Yet another studio experiment off the beaten path, worth hearing for anyone who loves Hendrix. ****e ARE YOU EXPERIENCED? classic from 1967, save for the red hot Hendrix solo. It's looser than the album version, thanks especially to the confident, swinging and colorful playing of drummer Mitch Mitchell in a style he developed in live settings. ****  \\"Red House\\" - a fine, extended reading of Hendrix's classic blues original. ****1/2  \\"Lullaby for the Summer\\" - is a cool, rangey instrumental that would eventually evolve into \\"Ezy Rider.\\" Stands as a unique piece of studio experimentation. ***1/2  \\"Crying Blue Rain\\" - begins as a soulful instrumental that picks up the pace towards the end of the song. Richards provides a ton of interesting details about his life, including the evolution of The Rolling Stones, his relationship with his family and the other members of the band, and even briefly touches on some of the guitar techniques and modifications that make his sound unique. Granted, Cinderella had to stand next to Prince Charming, whereas Belle's comparison is the \\"horrible, hideous Beast!\\"    The story, in short (spoiler alert! - but as in most musicals, the plot is not really the point) - Belle's father (Maurice) seeks shelter from a wolf attack in a strange castle, inhabited by enchanted objects - Lumiere the candlestick, Cogsworth the clock, Mrs. Potts the teapot, etc.  The castle had been enchanted because the master of the castle, now an enormous, angry beast, was unkind and treated a disguised enchantress badly.  Belle finds Maurice in the castle, sick and dying in the dungeon, and trades her life for her father's.  Through the help of the servants (the enchanted objects), Beast and Belle learn to live together and even to enjoy each others' company.  Meanwhile, Gaston, the local narcisistic bully has decided he will do anything to marry Belle (\\"In town there's only she/Who is beautiful as me/So I'm making plans to woo and marry Belle\\").  Ultimately Gaston and the Beast do battle, and Belle's love redeems the Beast and breaks the enchantment.    Thus, on the surface, this is a standard Disney adventure, good vs. evil, seen in everything before it from Snow White to The Little Mermaid.  However, there are a few key points that make this film rise above all the others.  First is the quality of the songs, and their integration in the plot.  They are catchy tunes but also efficient storytellers, especially the \\"Something There\\" montage that shows Beast and Belle turning from a state of mutual dislike to one of friendship.  There is also a clear redemption story parallelling the good-vs.-evil story, which is rare in Disney films (only Ratatouille, the Lion King, and Cars have such an integration of a redemption story, and of those only Lion King can compete with Beauty and the Beast for sheer emotional effectiveness).  The third key is Belle herself - she is a true hero and makes her own happy ending.  Let us compare her to the other classic Disney Princesses: Aurora is never given the opportunity for heroism and is simply rescued by Prince Philip, Cinderella suffers much but has given up to dispair when the Fairy Godmother finally appears to rescue her, Ariel selfishly and foolishly jeopardizes her own life and her father's kingdom, etc.  Snow White does bravely pull herself together and rescues herself when cast out by the Queen, but she then naively allows herself to get poisoned (and again a Prince needs to rescue her).  Only Mulan shows a degree of moral fortitude similar to Belle when she, too, saves her incapacitated father's life by taking his place (but, as pointed out in another Amazon review, who remembers any of the songs in Mulan?).    So, given that you simply MUST buy this film, the question becomes Blu-ray or DVD-only?  (in these Diamond editions, the Blu-ray also comes with the DVD)  I'm not sure which of these extras are included in the DVD-only version, but the full suite of Blu-ray extras are impressive (second only to the greatest Blu-ray I've ever seen, which is Snow White).  There are many many extras detailing the making of the film (and of the spin-off Broadway musical).  Of particular interest to me is the degree to which this is actually Howard Ashman's film.  Sure, he is listed as Executive Producer, but that could mean anything.  In this case, it appears he exterted the kind of control that, say, George Lucas did in Return of the Jedi.  He even ruthlessly cut one of his own songs when it wasn't working and replaced it with \\"Something More\\".  The excised song - \\"Human Again\\" - is seamlessly re-integrated into this Blu-ray (you can watch the \\"Original Theatrical Version\\" or the \\"Extended Version\\" which includes the new song).  It's fully animated and (since it was recorded before it was cut) contains the original voice actors' singing.  There is the original 20-minute \\"treatment\\" that was shown to Katzenberger and the other Disney executives (which led to a change in the creative leadership of the film)  Finally, like in Snow White, several extra animated shorts from Walt Disney himself are included - two Silly Symphonies and four Laugh-o-Gram fairytales (including a 7-minute Cinderella).  Unfortunately, it is difficult to find some of these special features as they are buried under multiple menus of non-intuitive location.  Even more frustrating (indeed, almost enraging) are the Blu-ray technical issues that have plagued every Disney Blu-ray yet produced: there is no \\"resume play\\" feature - if you stop half-way through you can't re-start from where you stopped, you even have to watch the anti-piracy warnings again.  You cannot even fast-search through the special features!  Are you telling me that my $30 Walmart DVD player has these features but my $400 Sony-built PS3 does not?  Even my old VCR  from 1980 could fast-search....  Exactly when will Blu-ray technology manage to be able to do the same technical things that were common 30 years ago?

0316098337|5.0|The premise of Emma Donoghue's novel, Room, is certainly off-putting. The child of a young woman and her kidnapper is being raised by his mother in an eleven-by-eleven foot prison located in the kidnapper's backyard.  And the kidnapper has visiting privileges.  Jolly, yes?  Ok, so now that you know the worst, I can tell you why you should read this remarkable novel.  The novel is narrated by the child, Jack, which could be annoying, but in Donoghue's capable hands is brilliant.   From the limited perspective of a five-year-old, all seems well in Jack's world.  He is happy.  He has an extensive if uneven vocabulary and an active imagination.  He loves Dora the Explorer  and hates green beans.   He and his \\"Ma\\" have a close relationship. She maintains a daily regime for him that includes Phys Ed, reading, drawing, and nap time.  But early on, despite the seemingly normal activities, we realize something is not quite right about Jack's home life.   Ma never talks about her past or explains to her son that they are actually prisoners in Room. For Jack, Room is the entire world, along with Wardrobe, Rocker, Skylight, and Lamp. But it becomes increasingly hard for his mother to keep up the illusion.  Ma knows the time has come to explain and escape.  Suddenly at age five, everything Jack thought he knew about home, identity, and reality is called into question.  An exquisite novel about love and the meaning of existence that transcends the subject matter.

B0049OSQ18|4.0|\\"Uh oh,\\" I thought right after I first dropped the needle on the new Decemberists' album, The King Is Dead. \\"This sounds like `You Don't Know How It Feels' by Tom Petty\\".<br /><br />When I first heard that song when back in 1994, I thought, \\"Tom Petty is obviously trying to sound like Neil Young...who was himself trying to sound like Bob Dylan.\\" Young trying to sound like Dylan is inspired. But The Decemberists sounding like Tom Petty was a portentous beginning for the sixth full-length release by one of the two or three best new bands of the new millennium's first decade.<br /><br />Fortunately, \\"Don't Carry It All\\" sounds like Petty for only about a single bar. Then The Decemberists get to the point of The King Is Dead: stripping away all of the prog-rock obsessions and mining different musical territory. According to front man Colin Meloy, R.E.M. was his biggest influence when writing and recording this album.<br /><br />In order to assure the authenticity of this claim, Peter Buck was good enough to play on the opening track and its follow-up, \\"Calamity Song\\". On the later, the guitar is so obviously Buck's that one almost need not read the credits to be certain that it is. However, the foot-stomping inspiring beat is also reminiscent of early R.E.M. songs like \\"Sitting Still\\", \\"7 Chinese Bros.\\", and \\"Pretty Persuasion\\". The lyrics, on the other hand, are pure Meloy, his tongue firmly in his cheek when he follows \\"Queen of supply-side bonhomie bone-drab\\" with a parenthetical \\"Know what I mean?\\"<br /><br />\\"Rise To Me\\" slows down the record's tempo a bit, and guitarist Chris Funk plays a fine pedal steel guitar. It also addresses someone named Henry, which, as music critic Greg Kot (Chicago Tribune) smartly notes in his review, is the name of Meloy's young son. Then the album picks up without missing a beat for \\"Rox In the Box\\", a dark but enjoyably existential sing-a-long which will please longtime fans with its use of according and violin. \\"January Hymn\\" ends the first half of the album on a melancholy note, and includes rueful the lyric, \\"What were the words I meant to say before you left?\\" (This and \\"June Hymm\\", which appears on the second half of the album, recall some of Richard Thompson's slower numbers.)<br /><br />Then comes the first single from The King Is Dead, the uptempo \\"Down By the Water\\". This song also features Peter Buck on guitar, as well as the wonderful refrain \\"The season rubs me wrong/The summer swells anon\\". R.E.M.'s influence is as obvious here as it is on \\"Calamity Song\\". As always, fortunately, Colin Meloy is clearly inspired by his influences. He does not simply imitate or copy them. Fans of both R.E.M. and The Decemberists, of whom I hope that there are many, will be happy to hear the two forces at work.<br /><br />No sooner is the influence of Athens, Georgia's finest obvious then comes \\"All Arise!\\", on which echoes of The Band--whose spirit weaves its way throughout the record--are also apparent. While this is an inspired effort, it is not one of the album's better songs. Meanwhile, \\"This Is Why We Fight\\" is lyrically less potent than it could have been, but Meloy choses his words economically, and the hard-rocking guitars and percussion help drive home the song's anti-war message.<br /><br />I first came to truly appreciate The Decemberists with The Crane Wife, the band's fourth full-length album. I was working in a record store, and the guy working behind the counter put it on. Although I was born in 1976, I was a huge fan of English progressive rock, especially Jethro Tull, in high school. (No, I was not the coolest guy in my class.) This helped make The Crane Wife the perfect place for me to belatedly become a devoted fan of The Decemberists.<br /><br />After working my way through their albums and EPs, with Picaresque (2005) and The Crane Wife standing as twin columns of brilliance, I was convinced--even a bit worried--that The Decemberists could do no wrong. Fortunately, I learned thatMeloy and company were mere mortals with the ambitious 2009 album The Hazards of Love. Don't get me wrong: that was a very good album, and was the next logical step in The Decemberist's creative growth. However, it was also the band's first album since its 2002 debut (Castaways and Cutouts) that did not hold together as seamlessly as their other ones.<br /><br />The King Is Dead, like The Hazards of Love, demonstrates that Colin Meloy--with the support of his band of top-shelf musicians--can achieve whatever he sets out to. It is not as good as Picaresque or The Crane Wife, but even when the songs on it are not great, they are admirably executed given Meloy's goals for the album.<br /><br />(Oh yeah, and you are less likely to need a dictionary to understand the lyrics this time.)Meloy and company were mere mortals with the ambitious 2009 album The Hazards of Love. Don't get me wrong: that was a very good album, and was the next logical step in The Decemberist's creative growth. However, it was also the band's first album since its 2002 debut (Castaways and Cutouts) that did not hold together as seamlessly as their other ones.<br /><br />The King Is Dead, like The Hazards of Love, demonstrates that Colin Meloy--with the support of his band of top-shelf musicians--can achieve whatever he sets out to. 0316010669|4.0|Everyone makes snap judgments about people, places, and events. Sometimes those judgments are correct, other times not. But what if you're forced to make a big decision in a matter of seconds? What processes in your brain help you make that decision, and how do you prevent bad decisions from happening?<br /><br />Through fascinating studies and real life examples, Malcom Gladwell explores these concepts and much more in this book. As Gladwell states, there are three purposes to the book. First, to show that quick decisions can be just as good as decisions made after slow, careful deliberation. Second, to identify when we should trust our instincts and when we should be wary of them; and third, to persuade the reader that snap judgments and first impressions can be educated and controlled.<br /><br />To demonstrate Gladwell's points, plenty of pages are devoted to studies and examples that made the book sound like a marketing textbook at times. Descriptions of the many types of facial expressions were so detailed that it became monotonous. Also, I would have liked more tips about how to control snap decisions, but the book certainly gave me a better understanding of why and how first impressions are made. Having said this, Blink is a worthwhile read, and the concepts will compel you to reevaluate those snap decisions you make in your life.

It's a wonder it doesn't collapse in on its own pretension and turn into a black hole. And yet... I rather liked it. Oh, it's far from a great movie. But it has a certain amount of charm.    The plot, such as it is, centers around two rival dance troupes. They have silly names and call themselves \\"houses\\". One is lead by the handsome and charismatic Luke while the other is lead by the handsome and charismatic Julian. We know Julian is Eeeeevil because he wears black and has a sinister look about him. They take their dancing so seriously I kept having flashbacks to the \\"South Park\\" episode \\"You Got F'd in the A\\".    Into their world wanders a boy with the unfortunate nickname of Moose. Apparently he was in the other films, where I sincerely hope his nickname gets explained. He's played by Adam G Sevani, a very attractive, talented young dancer who gives the distinct impression of a gay person trying to play straight and falling short. He's cute and charming and very good at dancing.    He gets recruited into Luke's little dance troupe and we soon find out that Luke's home/rehearsal space is about to be foreclosed on. Thankfully there's a dance competition coming up soon and the prize is $100,000. It's astonishing to me that could possibly be enough to cover a mortgage on a large space in New York City, but whatever.    The plot goes from there with dancing, betrayal, dancing, romance, dancing and dancing, with only enough plot as needed to move the film forward. Really it reminded me of one of those porn films that pretends to have a plot when really all the audience wants is for the movie to do what it's meant to do. It's much the same here. I would have been far more interested in two hours of dancing footage and zero plot.    Speaking of the dancing, oh, goodness. Yes, it's quite impressive. There's several scenes where we see dancing but it's so heavily edited it's very hard to tell exactly what's going on and to follow the action. That's a shame, because there's some real talent, but the camera doesn't take enough time to showcase it. This is less of a problem toward the end of the film, but it's a major issue at the start.    Ignoring that and the really, really stupid plot, this is a fun little movie. If you can just put your brain into neutral and coast along, you'll be just fine.

B00005K3O4|5.0|KILLER KLOWNS FROM OUTER SPACE is a clever, fast-moving scary/comedy that takes place during the course of an evening in Santa Cruz, California.  The movie was filmed in Santa Cruz, California, location of an amusement park, but in the storyline, the location is anywhere in the United States.  The movie is a complete original, but one can find similarities in THE BLOB featuring Steve McQueen (teenaged boy) and Aneta Corsaut (teenaged girl), ALIEN TRESSPASS featuring Eric McCormack (scientist), Jenni Baird (young waitress), and Robert Patrick (sleazy policeman), and INVASION OF THE BODY SNATCHERS (1956).  (Please do not bother with the color version of INVASION OF THE BODY SNATCHERS (1978), because it is decidedly inferior to the original.)  One might also find similarities in the many films where clowns are depicted as pernicious and evil. The special effects and the music in KILLER KLOWNS FROM OUTER SPACE are unexpectedly good.<br /><br />GENERAL PREMISE.  KILLER KLOWNS begins with teenagers making out in their automobiles, just as in the opening sequences in THE BLOB and ALIEN TRESPASS.  The heros of the story, Mike (boy) and Debbie (girl), noticed a shooting star, investigate, and discover a circus tent.  They go inside, and discover that it is really a rocketship, complete with colorful buttons, bubble chambers, a vast hexagonal chamber several stories tall, and pink pods.  The pink pods are made of cotton candy, but inside are human beings being incubated.  During the course of the movie, the clowns amble into town, entice the townsfolks, and play various tricks on the townsfolks and then zap them with ray guns.  Mike and Debbie spend much of the movie trying to convince the townspeople that they are in danger.  Eventually, Debbie is captured by the clowns, and Mike finally acquires the cooperation of a young policemen and two young men (Terenzi brothers) driving an ice cream truck, where the goal is to rescue Debbie.  The Terenzi brothers are a recurring themein this film, but they eventually prove crucial to the resolving the danger of the Killer Klowns.<br /><br />CLEVER IDEAS.  The movie overflows with an abundance of ideas.  Here are a few:<br /><br />(1) After Mike and Debbie discover the circus tent spaceship and explore the interior, they are discovered by the Killer Klowns and they run out and escape into the forest.  The Killer Klowns follow them, with the help of bloodhounds.  But the bloodhounds are really colorful balloons that are twisted into the shape of dogs (we have all seen clowns making twisted balloon animals at parties).  The balloon-dogs act like real dogs, and run, and sniff the air.<br /><br />(2) A clown walks down mainstreet.  It wants to avoid detection by shoppers.  The clown spots a motorized gorilla in front of a drugstore, waving its arms slowly around.  The clown then stands near the gorilla, and mimicks the gorilla's arm-waving actions, thereby avoiding detection.<br /><br />(3) A clown sets up a little puppet shown in the town square.  A teenager views the puppet show, which features a boy puppet and a girl puppet.  The boy puppet wants to get romantic with the girl puppet, but the girl puppet pulls out a ray gun and vaporizes the boy puppet.  (The teenager laughs at this.)  Then, a Killer Klown stands up, holding the girl puppet and the ray gun, and the Killer Klown vaporizes the teenager.<br /><br />(4) A small Killer Klown rides a toy motorcycle into a dark alley, where a motorcycle gang is meandering and loitering.  The small Killer Klown parks his little motorcycle, and one of the gang members decides to have fun and pick on the small Killer Klown.  The gang member says, \\"What are you going to do?  Knock my block off?\\"  Then, this is what happens, the small Killer Klown actually knocks the man's head off, and it lands in a trash bucket, and the headless body collapses.<br /><br />(5)  In a scene at a burger restaurant, a Killer Klown stands near the door, beckoning to a 6-year oldgirl who is inside dining with her mom.  The 6-year old girl approaches the door.  The Killer Klown plays peek-a-boo with his hands covering and uncovering his eyes, in an attempt to attract the girl.  (Whoever wrote the screenplay has a high degree of awareness of little children.  This scene was very, very accurate in this sense, and scary too.)<br /><br />(6)  A group of elderly people, perhaps in their 80s, are waiting at a bus stop at night.  A Killer Klown begins to entertain them with hand-shadows.  The old people are charmed.  They smile and laugh.  Then, the Killer Klown makes a shadow-puppet of a tyranosaurus rex, and the shadow eats the old people.  Gulp!<br /><br />THE PLOT MOVES FORWARD.  In addition to the clever episodes disclosed above, the storyline features Dave (young policeman and protagonist) and Mooney (obnoxious older policeman).  The teenagers complain to Mooney about the Killer Klowns.  Mooney receives various phone calls at his police desk regarding the Killer Klowns.  But Mooney dismisses all of these as pranks.  Mooney curses at the teenagers.  But eventually, one of the Killer Klowns pays a late-night visit to Mooney, and that's it for Mooney.  At about this time, Debbie is shown getting undressed at home in her bathroom for a shower.  Nothing X-rated is shown.  There are not even any cheesecake scenes.  The bathroom scene is entirely appropriate for the juvenile audience. (So don't worry, parents!) As the plot moves along, the storyline returns now and then to the bathroom and Debbie, and eventually the Killer Klowns overcome Debbie in her bathroom and encase her in a large yellow balloon, and bring her to their rocketship tent.<br /><br />Another recurring theme is where the protagonists (Mike and Dave) encounter more and more of the cotton candy pods.  Yet another recurring theme -- more a running gag -- is the Terenzi brothers, and their goal of using a large ice cream truck as a way to attract girls, so that they can find girlfriends.  While the Terenzi brothers and their truck appear to be merely a running gag, they eventually provide crucial to resolving the problem of the Killer Klowns.  (I DO NOT GIVE AWAY THE ENDING.)<br /><br />CONCLUSION.  The various plots and sight gags are skillfully woven together.  The plot moves along with the velocity of hurricane.  The colors are abundantly splashy and a treat to the eye.  The interior of the circus tent rocketship is detailed, colorful, and was (in part) inspired a typical amusement park fun house. The movie is clever and well-organized in the same way that a Mozart sonata is clever and well-organized.  The only negative thing that I can say about this film is that the clowns are too scary for children under seven years old.

B00008CLOA|4.0|This is a technical review only on the merits of the sound quality and the mix itself. I give this release 4 stars for the sound quality, what you would expect from the high bit rate SACD format. Too bad this format did not survive - this is what the CD should have been. The high end sparkles, good bass and no mud. Some of the vocals are a bit shrill but that may have been distortion on the source tape itself. BY the way, you need an SACD player to play this disc. I have the Sony DVP-NS775V which is a great model from around 2005.<br /><br />From my perspective having owned four different formats of this classic Floyd album since the early 70's, no one could improve upon the original 2 channel and 4 channel mixes, which Alan Parsons worked his magic on. The 2 channel mix here passes- I didn't notice anything glaring. But I am disappointed with the Surround mix. Compared with my original Quad tape from the 70's, this mix is too blended for my taste. So instead of discrete instruments panned to their own channels, mostly what you get is a blended ordeal. For example, while the bass, vocals and drums are prominent in the front, about 20% of that is mixed to the rears on every tune. Now you and I know that most audio setups have lesser quality speakers in the rear, and we should not have any bass or main drums going back there. There are some good moments here though such as the sax solos (front only), some background vocals (rear only), the 'lunatic' and a few instruments here and there being isolated to one or 2 speakers. The mixing engineer James Guthrie did manage to keep Parsons' brilliant Money cash register sequence intact - sounds emanate from all 4 corners just as the original Quad mix. Surround mix engineers who blend like this must assume we're going to play a surround disc without some of our speakers connected! This was the same deal on a few (but not all) the recent Genesis (dts) Surround releases- they missed a great opportunity there too. Want to hear some great, discrete Quad mixes - check out several early Moody Blues available on Hybrid SACD or Steely Dan's Gaucho on DTS (nothing in the rears but horns, backing vocals, percussion, some guitar riffs and organ - now that's what I'm talking about).<br /><br />Still, the Surround mix presented here is probably the only one available today, the sound quality is high end and this is more satisfying than listening to a dry stereo mix. So go for it and enjoy.
