0525951652|4.0|Overall I enjoyed Ken Follett's \\"Fall of Giants\\" and look forward to the second installment in his century trilogy.  Most of the fictional characters in the book (Earl Fitzherbert, Lady Maud, Ethel and Billy Williams, Grigori and Lev Peshkov, Gus Dewar, and so on) are believable in the sense they could have actually existed in real life and, if they did exist, would probably behave the way they do in the novel. The weak link in my opinion is  probably the German/Austrian Von Ulrich family, especially the young Walter, who is not that credible  from a historical perspective.     Generally speaking, for narrative purposes, fictional characters in the book are frequently involved in actual historical events interacting with real-life historical characters like Lloyd George, Winston Churchill, Woodrow Wilson, Vladimir Lenin and others. Barring that, which is inevitable in any work of fiction, Follett is reasonably faithful to historical fact and gives readers a pretty accurate description of European and, to a lesser extent, American society and politics in the 1911-1923 period, going through the traumatic events of World War I, the Russian Revolution and the early days of the Weimar Republic. Having said that, Follett's obsession to depict the European landed aristocracy as the \\"villains\\"  behind the war and, conversely, to glorify the working masses as both the innocent victims of that villainy and the heroes of the post-war brave new world is somewhat contrived.     The role of the aristocracy in the events that unfold in the book is particularly exaggerated in the case of the United Kingdom. Yes, Britain was a monarchy in 1914 (as it still is today !) and did have its own share of  hereditary dukes, earls, viscounts and barons, whose descendants are still around by the way and who, like Earl Fitzherbert in the book,  were wealthy landowners. By 1911 however, political power had been already transferred from the aristocracy to the upper middle-class as the House of Lords was effectively stripped of its power to veto legislation passed by the popularly elected House of Commons. Starting in the early 20th century, all British prime ministers and most of the key cabinet ministers who ran the daily affairs of the empire (e.g. finance, foreign relations, defense, etc.) were drawn from the Commons and did not hold peerages while in office. Likewise, senior civil servants (i.e professional government bureaucrats) and most military officers/commanders were also recruited from the middle classes, and not from the landed nobility.     That is not to say that Britain was a fully-fledged democracy by 1914. As mentioned in the book, parliamentary suffrage was somewhat narrow: Follett claims that only roughly 50 % of the adult males had the right to vote before WWI, although 60 % is probably a more accurate figure. In any case, a significant section of the working class and, most importantly, all women were excluded from the political process. One of the major topics in the book is precisely how the war led to the introduction of universal adult male suffrage and limited voting rights  (conditioned on certain age and property requirements) for women (women wouldn't get equal voting rights as men in Britain until 1928 !).      Although his interpretation may be over-simplistic, Follett makes a deliberate effort in the book to contrast the post-war fates of Britain on one hand, and  Russia/Germany on the other. Britain, victorious in the war, but economically crippled and facing domestic social unrest, did not descend into revolutionary chaos precisely because, as Follett argues, the political aspirations of the working classes and the trade union movement were ultimately accommodated by the parliamentary system with the rise of the Labour (U.S. spelling 'Labor') party. That eventually allowed a \\"socialist\\" government to co-exist even with the King and the hereditary peers, not to mention the ruling capitalist class. Follett, having been himself married to a former Labour member of Parliament, seems very comfortable describing the rise of the British Labour party to power in the general election of 1923, where both of his working-class sibling characters, Billy and Ethel, are elected to the House of Commons (a truly socialist Labour government wouldn't take over in the UK though until the general election of 1945 !).       By contrast, in the continent, as the autocracy of the Tsar and the Kaiser were swept away by the war, the lack of properly functioning democratic institutions that could accommodate social tension contributed to the early rise of communism in Russia in the 1917-21 period and, later, to the Nazis seizing power in Germany in the 1930's. Follett will no doubt elaborate more on this as his second book in the trilogy guides us through the 1930s, World War II and the horrors of Nazism and Stalinism.     Follett's depiction of America, the rising global power in the early 20th century that was destined to replace the old European empires as the world's most influential country, is also curious. Compared to his own sober and overtly negative description of the European societies,  Follett paints a mostly positive and upbeat picture of the United States. Given the author's own aforementioned anti-aristocracy bias,  it is unsurprising that he would see the United States, a federal republic without a landed nobility, as a comparatively more democratic and egalitarian society. That was BTW the predominant view  probably shared at the time by most European immigrants,  especially those from socially backward countries like Follett's own Lev Peshkov,  who were attracted to the United States not only because of its idea of freedom, but also, more significantly, because of its promise of material wealth and economic prosperity.     Follett, however, also strives to make the point that America's \\"equality\\" and \\"freedom\\" only went so far. Not only was there a social class divide in the United States (as seen in Follett's Buffalo and as expected in any capitalist society), but also, more significantly, as the book stresses, \\"equality\\" did not extend to African-Americans (\\"negroes\\"), who, at the time, were legally segregated and, in many states, effectively barred from voting due to the use of poll taxes, literacy tests and other similar mechanisms. Even U.S. president Woodrow Wilson, the idealist intellectual who came up with the 14 points for peace, is paradoxically described by Follett as an overt racist who introduced segregation in the federal government and suppressed free speech during the war with the Espionage Act. The social problems of America, including the early rise of gangsterism and organized crime following the introduction of alcohol prohibition, are also vividly and competently described in \\"Fall of Giants\\".     Just to conclude, perhaps the book's biggest shortcoming in my humble opinion is that we do not get to see the French point of view, as there is no major French character in the novel. Given that France was the biggest contributor to the allied forces in the western front (fought mostly on French soil) and probably had the most casualties in WWI after Germany and Russia, that omission is regrettable. In any case, like most English speakers, Follett probably sees the French as \\"irrelevant\\", so we, non-anglophone readers, have to live with that !    PS: Other Brazilians who might be reading this review may be interested to know that \\"Fall of Giants\\" has been translated to Portuguese under the title \\"Queda de Gigantes\\". Surprisingly, the original book in English is actually cheaper than the translated paperback version in Portuguese.

B00006J9OT|5.0|Since Canadian jazz singer/pianist Diana Krall burst on the popular music scene around two decades ago, I've always been amazed that much of the \\"buzz\\" about her has centered on her singing.  What I will say about her singing is that she makes the best of a limited range and less than stellar vocal clarity.  In voice, she is bested by her fellow Canadian pianist/vocalist Carol Welsman or 16-year old (in 2011) Canadian singer Nikki Yanofsky--both have more pleasant voices, better vocal clarity, and both can swing a tune vocally on par with Ms Krall.  Certainly, Ms. Krall's singing is passable and better than about 90% of the pop slop out there and that may account for some of her vocal popularity.  Some of Ms. Krall's albums have pandered too much to those popular audiences to suit this jazz purist, as well.  Fortunately, this album does not do that and this, in my opinion, is her best album to date.    If Ms. Krall's singing is less than stellar, the same can not be said for her piano playing.  In fact, she is without doubt one of the best, hardest swinging jazz pianists alive today.  If she never sang another note, she would still be a top jazz lion.  (A jazz musician friend is more direct about it--he says that Ms. Krall shouldn't sing at all because it distracts the listener from her superb jazz chops on the piano.  He adds that if Diana the singer and Diana the pianist were two people, Diana the singer would be angry at Diana the piano player for stealing the show.)  The song choices on this album are also excellent--mostly jazz standards that suit Ms. Krall's playing perfectly.  Another huge highlight of this album is her band--an all-star group of swinging musicians.  Unlike some vocalists/pianists, Ms. Krall is not afraid to feature them prominently during the course of this album.  The audio qulaity of this albume is excellent, too--exceptionally good for a live recording.  All in all, this album is best characterized as a first-rate jazz pianist and supporting group making a passable singer look pretty darned good.    One final note--having reviewed Canadian singer Nikki Yanofsky's two albums recently, I have to pose this question:  How is it that a country with a population smaller than that of California consistently produces a disproportionately large share of fine jazz musicians and popular singers compared to what we in the United States seem to be able to muster these days?  For me, that is an open question . . .

0307387178|4.0|In Jon Krakauer's riveting account of Chris McCandless's odyssey to find himself, the author does an amazing job in collecting evidence and portraying the life of a young man out to get away from society. Into the Wild is set over the course of three years around the country as Krakauer retraces the steps of McCandless's cross country trek. The year is 1991 and young Chris McCandless, a promising young man, is fed up with the materialistic values that framed both he and his emerging generation. Ever the iconoclast, he graduates from Emory with honors and immediately sets out to find his inner self by living off the land while baptizing himself with a new name, \\"Magic Alex.\\" In response to people who had gone \\"into the wild\\" before him, Chris embarks on the journey of a life time, only to be found dead in the wilds of Alaska in an abandoned bus ten miles from a road along the famed Alaskan Stampede Trail. Krakauer takes you on the life and death adventure of McCandless as he pieces together the fascinating journey of this young man who left behind an invaluable diary that forms the basis of Krakauer's research. The people Chris encounter will never forget this astonishing, idealistic young man, an individual so determined and so focused on his goal that he never left the minds of those he impacted. McCandless was not your ordinary hitchhiker, nearly every person he met on his \\"Great Alaskan Odyssey\\" noticed how noticeably bright this kid was. One man who picked McCandless up on his final journey to Fairbanks, comments to Krakauer, \\"`Alex was clean-shaven and had short hair, and I could tell by the language he used that he was a real sharp fella. He wasn't what you'd call a typical hitchhiker. I'm usually leery of'em. I figure there's probably something wrong with a guy if he can't even afford a bus ticket.'\\" (159). This was what was different about McCandless, he was a smart young man and yet he made some serious blunders in his logic and reasoning. Krakauer did hisresearch on this one as he stitched together a web of first-hand encounters and experiences with Chris McCandless, who was hard to follow as he changed his name every place he went. Many people who read Chris's story call him insane and others call him a purist gone too far. Krakauer makes sure that you can come to your own conclusions on the mental state and reasons for Chris's erratic behavior. I see it as this: a boy turned man fed up with society and trying to get away from it all ventures cross-country and tries to survive off the land in the wilderness of Alaska. I do not believe he is crazy, just enormously driven and focus-oriented; in the end, he wants to live off the land in the wilderness and would not take no for an answer, even if it meant endangering himself. Overall, Jon Krakauer fashions a genuine winner with his remembrance, Into the Wild. The tome combines the information of a news article and autobiography with the interesting and enjoyable reading of a novel. I found this tract to be very interesting and an overall great read. I definitely recommend this book as reading Krakauer's account of a young man out to find himself and break free from the norms of society is interesting and overall it is a great volume that is truly hard to put down.
